</div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Types">\n        Types\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Types">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_types" title="Precipitation types">\n        Precipitation types\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FoggDam-NT.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="1000" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FoggDam-NT.jpg/220px-FoggDam-NT.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FoggDam-NT.jpg/330px-FoggDam-NT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FoggDam-NT.jpg/440px-FoggDam-NT.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FoggDam-NT.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A thunderstorm with heavy precipitation\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Precipitation is a major component of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">\n        water cycle\n       </a>\n       , and is responsible for depositing most of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">\n        fresh water\n       </a>\n       on the\n       <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">\n        planet\n       </a>\n       . Approximately 505,000\xc2\xa0km\n       <sup>\n        3\n       </sup>\n       (121,000\xc2\xa0mi\n       <sup>\n        3\n       </sup>\n       ) of water falls as precipitation each year, 398,000\xc2\xa0km\n       <sup>\n        3\n       </sup>\n       (95,000\xc2\xa0cu\xc2\xa0mi) of it over the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">\n        oceans\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_4-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-chow-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Given the\n       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">\n        Earth\n       </a>\n       \'s surface area, that means the globally averaged annual precipitation is 990 millimetres (39\xc2\xa0in).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Mechanisms of producing precipitation include convective,\n       <a href="/wiki/Stratus_cloud" title="Stratus cloud">\n        stratiform\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">\n        orographic\n       </a>\n       rainfall.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Convective processes involve strong vertical motions that can cause the overturning of the atmosphere in that location within an hour and cause heavy precipitation,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convection_8-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-convection-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       while stratiform processes involve weaker upward motions and less intense precipitation.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Precipitation can be divided into three categories, based on whether it falls as liquid water, liquid water that freezes on contact with the surface, or ice. Mixtures of different types of precipitation, including types in different categories, can fall simultaneously. Liquid forms of precipitation include rain and drizzle. Rain or drizzle that freezes on contact within a subfreezing\n       <a href="/wiki/Air_mass" title="Air mass">\n        air mass\n       </a>\n       is called "freezing rain" or "freezing drizzle". Frozen forms of precipitation include\n       <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">\n        snow\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Diamond_dust" title="Diamond dust">\n        ice needles\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_pellet" title="Ice pellet">\n        ice pellets\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">\n        hail\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Graupel" title="Graupel">\n        graupel\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">\n        <a href="#cite_note-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="How_the_air_becomes_saturated">\n        How the air becomes saturated\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: How the air becomes saturated">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cooling_air_to_its_dew_point">\n        Cooling air to its dew point\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cooling air to its dew point">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regnbyge.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="859" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Regnbyge.jpg/220px-Regnbyge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Regnbyge.jpg/330px-Regnbyge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Regnbyge.jpg/440px-Regnbyge.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regnbyge.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Late-summer\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainstorm" title="Rainstorm">\n          rainstorm\n         </a>\n         in\n         <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">\n          Denmark\n         </a>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg/220px-Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg/330px-Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg/440px-Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Lenticular cloud forming due to mountains over Wyoming\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">\n        dew point\n       </a>\n       is the temperature to which a parcel must be cooled in order to become saturated, and (unless super-saturation occurs) condenses to water.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Water vapour normally begins to condense on\n       <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">\n        condensation nuclei\n       </a>\n       such as dust, ice, and salt in order to form clouds. An elevated portion of a frontal zone forces broad areas of lift, which form clouds decks such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altostratus" title="Altostratus">\n        altostratus\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirrostratus" title="Cirrostratus">\n        cirrostratus\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Stratus_cloud" title="Stratus cloud">\n        Stratus\n       </a>\n       is a stable cloud deck which tends to form when a cool, stable air mass is trapped underneath a warm air mass. It can also form due to the lifting of\n       <a href="/wiki/Fog#Types" title="Fog">\n        advection fog\n       </a>\n       during breezy conditions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       There are four main mechanisms for cooling the air to its dew point: adiabatic cooling, conductive cooling,\n       <a href="/wiki/Radiative_cooling" title="Radiative cooling">\n        radiational coolin\n       </a>\n       g, and evaporative cooling.\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate#Dry_adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">\n        Adiabatic cooling\n       </a>\n       occurs when air rises and expands.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The air can rise due to\n       <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">\n        convection\n       </a>\n       , large-scale atmospheric motions, or a physical barrier such as a mountain (\n       <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">\n        orographic lift\n       </a>\n       ). Conductive cooling occurs when the air comes into contact with a colder surface,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       usually by being blown from one surface to another, for example from a liquid water surface to colder land. Radiational cooling occurs due to the emission of\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">\n        infrared radiation\n       </a>\n       , either by the air or by the surface underneath.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Evaporative cooling occurs when moisture is added to the air through evaporation, which forces the air temperature to cool to its\n       <a href="/wiki/Wet-bulb_temperature" title="Wet-bulb temperature">\n        wet-bulb temperature\n       </a>\n       , or until it reaches saturation.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adding_moisture_to_the_air">\n        Adding moisture to the air\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Adding moisture to the air">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The main ways water vapour is added to the air are: wind convergence into areas of upward motion,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convection_8-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-convection-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       precipitation or virga falling from above,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">\n        <a href="#cite_note-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       daytime heating evaporating water from the surface of oceans, water bodies or wet land,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">\n        <a href="#cite_note-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       transpiration from plants,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">\n        <a href="#cite_note-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       cool or dry air moving over warmer water,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and lifting air over mountains.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_21-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-MT-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">\n        Formation\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Formation">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">\n        Water cycle\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_cycle.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="860" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/220px-Water_cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/330px-Water_cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/440px-Water_cycle.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_cycle.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Condensation and coalescence are important parts of the\n         <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">\n          water cycle\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Raindrops">\n        Raindrops\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Raindrops">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coalescence_(meteorology)" title="Coalescence (meteorology)">\n        Coalescence\n       </a>\n       occurs when water droplets fuse to create larger water droplets, or when water droplets freeze onto an ice crystal, which is known as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergeron_process" title="Bergeron process">\n        Bergeron process\n       </a>\n       . The fall rate of very small droplets is negligible, hence clouds do not fall out of the sky; precipitation will only occur when these coalesce into larger drops. When air turbulence occurs, water droplets collide, producing larger droplets. As these larger water droplets descend, coalescence continues, so that drops become heavy enough to overcome air resistance and fall as rain.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">\n        <a href="#cite_note-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Raindrops have sizes ranging from 0.1 millimetres (0.0039\xc2\xa0in) to 9 millimetres (0.35\xc2\xa0in) mean diameter, above which they tend to break up. Smaller drops are called cloud droplets, and their shape is spherical. As a raindrop increases in size, its shape becomes more oblate, with its largest cross-section facing the oncoming airflow. Contrary to the cartoon pictures of raindrops, their shape does not resemble a teardrop.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Intensity and duration of rainfall are usually inversely related, i.e., high intensity storms are likely to be of short duration and low intensity storms can have a long duration.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JS_24-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-JS-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-houze_25-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-houze-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Rain drops associated with melting hail tend to be larger than other rain drops.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The METAR code for rain is RA, while the coding for rain showers is SHRA.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METAR_27-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-METAR-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_pellets">\n        Ice pellets\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ice pellets">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">\n        Ice pellets\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg/220px-Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg/330px-Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg/440px-Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sleet_on_the_ground.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         An accumulation of ice pellets\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">\n        Ice pellets\n       </a>\n       or sleet are a form of precipitation consisting of small,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translucent" title="Translucent">\n        translucent\n       </a>\n       balls of ice. Ice pellets are usually (but not always) smaller than\n       <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">\n        hailstones\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       They often bounce when they hit the ground, and generally do not freeze into a solid mass unless mixed with\n       <a href="/wiki/Freezing_rain" title="Freezing rain">\n        freezing rain\n       </a>\n       . The\n       <a href="/wiki/METAR" title="METAR">\n        METAR\n       </a>\n       code for ice pellets is\n       <b>\n        PL\n       </b>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METAR_27-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-METAR-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Ice pellets form when a layer of above-freezing air exists with sub-freezing air both above and below. This causes the partial or complete melting of any snowflakes falling through the warm layer. As they fall back into the sub-freezing layer closer to the surface, they re-freeze into ice pellets. However, if the sub-freezing layer beneath the warm layer is too small, the precipitation will not have time to re-freeze, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Freezing_rain" title="Freezing rain">\n        freezing rain\n       </a>\n       will be the result at the surface. A temperature profile showing a warm layer above the ground is most likely to be found in advance of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">\n        warm front\n       </a>\n       during the cold season,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       but can occasionally be found behind a passing\n       <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">\n        cold front\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hail">\n        Hail\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hail">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">\n        Hail\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Granizo.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1192" data-file-width="1840" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Granizo.jpg/220px-Granizo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Granizo.jpg/330px-Granizo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Granizo.jpg/440px-Granizo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Granizo.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A large hailstone, about 6 centimetres (2.4\xc2\xa0in) in diameter\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Like other precipitation, hail forms in storm\n       <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">\n        clouds\n       </a>\n       when\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supercooled" title="Supercooled">\n        supercooled\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">\n        water\n       </a>\n       droplets freeze on contact with\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condensation_nuclei" title="Condensation nuclei">\n        condensation nuclei\n       </a>\n       , such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">\n        dust\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Dirt" title="Dirt">\n        dirt\n       </a>\n       . The storm\'s\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">\n        updraft\n       </a>\n       blows the hailstones to the upper part of the cloud. The updraft dissipates and the hailstones fall down, back into the updraft, and are lifted again. Hail has a diameter of 5 millimetres (0.20\xc2\xa0in) or more.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gloss_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-gloss-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Within METAR code, GR is used to indicate larger hail, of a diameter of at least 6.4 millimetres (0.25\xc2\xa0in). GR is derived from the French word gr\xc3\xaale. Smaller-sized hail, as well as snow pellets, use the coding of GS, which is short for the French word gr\xc3\xa9sil.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METAR_27-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-METAR-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Stones just larger than\n       <a href="/wiki/Golf_ball" title="Golf ball">\n        golf ball\n       </a>\n       -sized are one of the most frequently reported hail sizes.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hailstones can grow to 15 centimetres (6\xc2\xa0in) and weigh more than 500 grams (1\xc2\xa0lb).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">\n        <a href="#cite_note-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In large hailstones,\n       <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">\n        latent heat\n       </a>\n       released by further freezing may melt the outer shell of the hailstone. The hailstone then may undergo \'wet growth\', where the liquid outer shell collects other smaller hailstones.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The hailstone gains an ice layer and grows increasingly larger with each ascent. Once a hailstone becomes too heavy to be supported by the storm\'s updraft, it falls from the cloud.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Snowflakes">\n        Snowflakes\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Snowflakes">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Snowflake" title="Snowflake">\n        Snowflake\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1854" data-file-width="2004" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg/170px-Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg/255px-Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg/340px-Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snowflake_-_Microphotograph_by_artgeek.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Snowflake viewed in an optical microscope\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Snow crystals form when tiny\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supercool" title="Supercool">\n        supercooled\n       </a>\n       cloud droplets (about 10\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="\xce\x9cm">\n        \xce\xbcm\n       </a>\n       in diameter)\n       <a href="/wiki/Freezing" title="Freezing">\n        freeze\n       </a>\n       . Once a droplet has frozen, it grows in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">\n        supersaturated\n       </a>\n       environment. Because water droplets are more numerous than the ice crystals the crystals are able to grow to hundreds of\n       <a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">\n        micrometers\n       </a>\n       in size at the expense of the water droplets. This process is known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Wegener%E2%80%93Bergeron%E2%80%93Findeisen_process" title="Wegener\xe2\x80\x93Bergeron\xe2\x80\x93Findeisen process">\n        Wegener\xe2\x80\x93Bergeron\xe2\x80\x93Findeisen process\n       </a>\n       . The corresponding depletion of water vapour causes the droplets to evaporate, meaning that the ice crystals grow at the droplets\' expense. These large crystals are an efficient source of precipitation, since they fall through the atmosphere due to their mass, and may collide and stick together in clusters, or aggregates. These aggregates are snowflakes, and are usually the type of ice particle that falls to the ground.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeojan07_35-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-natgeojan07-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Guinness World Records list the world\'s largest snowflakes as those of January 1887 at Fort Keogh, Montana; allegedly one measured 38\xc2\xa0cm (15\xc2\xa0inches) wide.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">\n        <a href="#cite_note-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The exact details of the sticking mechanism remain a subject of research.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Although the ice is clear, scattering of light by the crystal facets and hollows/imperfections mean that the crystals often appear white in color due to\n       <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_reflection" title="Diffuse reflection">\n        diffuse reflection\n       </a>\n       of the whole\n       <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">\n        spectrum\n       </a>\n       of\n       <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">\n        light\n       </a>\n       by the small ice particles.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The shape of the snowflake is determined broadly by the temperature and humidity at which it is formed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeojan07_35-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-natgeojan07-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Rarely, at a temperature of around \xe2\x88\x922\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C (28\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0F), snowflakes can form in threefold symmetry\xe2\x80\x94triangular snowflakes.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The most common snow particles are visibly irregular, although near-perfect snowflakes may be more common in pictures because they are more visually appealing. No two snowflakes are alike,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       as they grow at different rates and in different patterns depending on the changing temperature and humidity within the atmosphere through which they fall on their way to the ground.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The METAR code for snow is SN, while snow showers are coded SHSN.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METAR_27-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-METAR-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diamond_dust">\n        Diamond dust\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Diamond dust">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Diamond_dust" title="Diamond dust">\n        Diamond dust\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Diamond dust, also known as ice needles or ice crystals, forms at temperatures approaching \xe2\x88\x9240\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x9240\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0F) due to air with slightly higher moisture from aloft mixing with colder, surface based air.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossdia_41-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-glossdia-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       They are made of simple ice crystals that are hexagonal in shape.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">\n        <a href="#cite_note-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The METAR identifier for diamond dust within international hourly weather reports is IC.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METAR_27-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-METAR-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">\n        Causes\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Causes">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Frontal_activity">\n        Frontal activity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Frontal activity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">\n        Weather fronts\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Stratiform or dynamic precipitation occurs as a consequence of slow ascent of air in\n       <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale_meteorology" title="Synoptic scale meteorology">\n        synoptic systems\n       </a>\n       (on the order of cm/s), such as over surface\n       <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">\n        cold fronts\n       </a>\n       , and over and ahead of\n       <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">\n        warm fronts\n       </a>\n       . Similar ascent is seen around\n       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">\n        tropical cyclones\n       </a>\n       outside of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">\n        eyewall\n       </a>\n       , and in comma-head precipitation patterns around\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-latitude_cyclone" title="Mid-latitude cyclone">\n        mid-latitude cyclones\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geerts_43-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Geerts-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A wide variety of weather can be found along an occluded front, with thunderstorms possible, but usually their passage is associated with a drying of the air mass. Occluded fronts usually form around mature low-pressure areas.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DR_44-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-DR-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Precipitation may occur on celestial bodies other than Earth. When it gets cold,\n       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">\n        Mars\n       </a>\n       has precipitation that most likely takes the form of ice needles, rather than rain or snow.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars_5-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mars-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Convection">\n        Convection\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Convection">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Konvektionsregen.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="610" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Konvektionsregen.jpg/220px-Konvektionsregen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Konvektionsregen.jpg/330px-Konvektionsregen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Konvektionsregen.jpg/440px-Konvektionsregen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Konvektionsregen.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Convective precipitation\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convection_rain" title="Convection rain">\n        Convective rain\n       </a>\n       , or showery precipitation, occurs from convective clouds, e.g.,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus" title="Cumulonimbus">\n        cumulonimbus\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_congestus" title="Cumulus congestus">\n        cumulus congestus\n       </a>\n       . It falls as showers with rapidly changing intensity. Convective precipitation falls over a certain area for a relatively short time, as convective clouds have limited horizontal extent. Most precipitation in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">\n        tropics\n       </a>\n       appears to be convective; however, it has been suggested that stratiform precipitation also occurs.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-houze_25-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-houze-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geerts_43-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Geerts-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Graupel" title="Graupel">\n        Graupel\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">\n        hail\n       </a>\n       indicate convection.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In mid-latitudes, convective precipitation is intermittent and often associated with baroclinic boundaries such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">\n        cold fronts\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">\n        squall lines\n       </a>\n       , and warm fronts.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">\n        <a href="#cite_note-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orographic_effects">\n        Orographic effects\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Orographic effects">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">\n        Orographic lift\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_types" title="Precipitation types">\n        Precipitation types\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steigungsregen.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="612" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Steigungsregen.jpg/220px-Steigungsregen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Steigungsregen.jpg/330px-Steigungsregen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Steigungsregen.jpg/440px-Steigungsregen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steigungsregen.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Orographic precipitation\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Orographic precipitation occurs on the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windward" title="Windward">\n        windward\n       </a>\n       side of mountains and is caused by the rising air motion of a large-scale flow of moist air across the mountain ridge, resulting in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">\n        adiabatic\n       </a>\n       cooling and condensation. In mountainous parts of the world subjected to relatively consistent winds (for example, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_wind" title="Trade wind">\n        trade winds\n       </a>\n       ), a more moist\n       <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">\n        climate\n       </a>\n       usually prevails on the windward side of a mountain than on the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leeward" title="Leeward">\n        leeward\n       </a>\n       or downwind side. Moisture is removed by orographic lift, leaving drier air (see\n       <a href="/wiki/Katabatic_wind" title="Katabatic wind">\n        katabatic wind\n       </a>\n       ) on the descending and generally warming, leeward side where a\n       <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">\n        rain shadow\n       </a>\n       is observed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_21-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-MT-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">\n        Hawaii\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Wai%CA%BBale%CA%BBale" title="Mount Wai\xca\xbbale\xca\xbbale">\n        Mount Wai\xca\xbbale\xca\xbbale\n       </a>\n       , on the island of Kauai, is notable for its extreme rainfall, as it has the second highest average annual rainfall on Earth, with 12,000 millimetres (460\xc2\xa0in).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Storm systems affect the state with heavy rains between October and March. Local climates vary considerably on each island due to their topography, divisible into windward (\n       <i>\n        Ko\n        <span class="unicode">\n         \xca\xbb\n        </span>\n        olau\n       </i>\n       ) and leeward (\n       <i>\n        Kona\n       </i>\n       ) regions based upon location relative to the higher mountains. Windward sides face the east to northeast\n       <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">\n        trade winds\n       </a>\n       and receive much more rainfall; leeward sides are drier and sunnier, with less rain and less cloud cover.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">\n        <a href="#cite_note-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In South America, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">\n        Andes\n       </a>\n       mountain range blocks Pacific moisture that arrives in that continent, resulting in a desertlike climate just downwind across western Argentina.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">\n        <a href="#cite_note-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">\n        Sierra Nevada\n       </a>\n       range creates the same effect in North America forming the\n       <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">\n        Great Basin\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">\n        Mojave Deserts\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">\n        <a href="#cite_note-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">\n        <a href="#cite_note-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Similarly, in Asia, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya_mountains" title="Himalaya mountains">\n        Himalaya mountains\n       </a>\n       create an obstacle to monsoons which leads to extremely high precipitation on the southern side and lower precipitation levels on the northern side.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Snow">\n        Snow\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Snow">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">\n        Snow\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       [[File:Snow Clouds in Korea.jpg|thumb|Lake-effect snow bands near the\n       <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">\n        Korean Peninsula\n       </a>\n       ] in early-December 2008.]\n       <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">\n        Extratropical cyclones\n       </a>\n       can bring cold and dangerous conditions with heavy rain and snow with winds exceeding 119\xc2\xa0km/h (74\xc2\xa0mph),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarinersWeatherLog_52-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-MarinersWeatherLog-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       (sometimes referred to as\n       <a href="/wiki/European_windstorm" title="European windstorm">\n        windstorms\n       </a>\n       in Europe). The band of precipitation that is associated with their\n       <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">\n        warm front\n       </a>\n       is often extensive, forced by weak upward vertical motion of air over the frontal boundary which\n       <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">\n        condenses\n       </a>\n       as it cools and produces precipitation within an elongated band,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">\n        <a href="#cite_note-53">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which is wide and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_types_(meteorology)#Stratiform" title="Precipitation types (meteorology)">\n        stratiform\n       </a>\n       , meaning falling out of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nimbostratus" title="Nimbostratus">\n        nimbostratus\n       </a>\n       clouds.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">\n        <a href="#cite_note-54">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       When moist air tries to dislodge an arctic air mass, overrunning snow can result within the poleward side of the elongated\n       <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">\n        precipitation band\n       </a>\n       . In the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">\n        Northern Hemisphere\n       </a>\n       , poleward is towards the\n       <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">\n        North Pole\n       </a>\n       , or north. Within the\n       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">\n        Southern Hemisphere\n       </a>\n       , poleward is towards the\n       <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">\n        South Pole\n       </a>\n       , or south.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Southwest of extratropical cyclones, curved cyclonic flow bringing cold air across the relatively warm water bodies can lead to narrow\n       <a href="/wiki/Lake-effect_snow" title="Lake-effect snow">\n        lake-effect snow\n       </a>\n       bands. Those bands bring strong localized snowfall which can be understood as follows: Large water bodies such as lakes efficiently store heat that results in significant temperature differences (larger than 13\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C or 23\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0F) between the water surface and the air above.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">\n        <a href="#cite_note-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Because of this temperature difference, warmth and moisture are transported upward, condensing into vertically oriented clouds (see satellite picture) which produce snow showers. The temperature decrease with height and cloud depth are directly affected by both the water temperature and the large-scale environment. The stronger the temperature decrease with height, the deeper the clouds get, and the greater the precipitation rate becomes.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">\n        <a href="#cite_note-56">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In mountainous areas, heavy snowfall accumulates when air is forced to ascend the mountains and squeeze out precipitation along their windward slopes, which in cold conditions, falls in the form of snow. Because of the ruggedness of terrain, forecasting the location of heavy snowfall remains a significant challenge.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">\n        <a href="#cite_note-57">\n         [57]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Within_the_tropics">\n        Within the tropics\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Within the tropics">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">\n        Wet season\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">\n        Monsoon\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">\n        Tropical cyclone\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       [[File:Cairns climate.svg|thumb|Rainfall distribution by month in\n       <a href="/wiki/Cairns" title="Cairns">\n        Cairns\n       </a>\n       showing the extent of the wet season at that location]] The wet, or rainy, season is the time of year, covering one or more months, when most of the average annual\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainfall" title="Rainfall">\n        rainfall\n       </a>\n       in a region falls.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">\n        <a href="#cite_note-58">\n         [58]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The term\n       <i>\n        green season\n       </i>\n       is also sometimes used as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Euphemism" title="Euphemism">\n        euphemism\n       </a>\n       by tourist authorities.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">\n        <a href="#cite_note-59">\n         [59]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Areas with wet seasons are dispersed across portions of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">\n        tropics\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">\n        subtropics\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">\n        <a href="#cite_note-60">\n         [60]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">\n        Savanna\n       </a>\n       climates and areas with\n       <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">\n        monsoon\n       </a>\n       regimes have wet summers and dry winters. Tropical rainforests technically do not have dry or wet seasons, since their rainfall is equally distributed through the year.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde_61-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hyde-61">\n         [61]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Some areas with pronounced rainy seasons will see a break in rainfall mid-season when the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertropical_convergence_zone" title="Intertropical convergence zone">\n        intertropical convergence zone\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_trough" title="Monsoon trough">\n        monsoon trough\n       </a>\n       move poleward of their location during the middle of the warm season.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JS_24-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-JS-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       When the wet season occurs during the warm season, or\n       <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">\n        summer\n       </a>\n       , rain falls mainly during the late afternoon and early evening hours. The wet season is a time when\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_quality" title="Air quality">\n        air quality\n       </a>\n       improves,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">\n        <a href="#cite_note-62">\n         [62]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       freshwater quality improves,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">\n        <a href="#cite_note-63">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">\n        <a href="#cite_note-64">\n         [64]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and vegetation grows significantly.\n       <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">\n        Soil\n       </a>\n       nutrients diminish and erosion increases.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JS_24-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-JS-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Animals have adaptation and survival strategies for the wetter regime. The previous dry season leads to food shortages into the wet season, as the crops have yet to mature. Developing countries have noted that their populations show seasonal weight fluctuations due to food shortages seen before the first harvest, which occurs late in the wet season.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marti_J._Van_Liere.2C_Eric-Alain_D._Ategbo.2C_Jan_Hoorweg.2C_Adel_P._Den_Hartog.2C_and_Joseph_G._A._J._Hautvast_1994_479.E2.80.93488_65-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Marti_J._Van_Liere.2C_Eric-Alain_D._Ategbo.2C_Jan_Hoorweg.2C_Adel_P._Den_Hartog.2C_and_Joseph_G._A._J._Hautvast_1994_479.E2.80.93488-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Tropical cyclones, a source of very heavy rainfall, consist of large air masses several hundred miles across with low pressure at the centre and with winds blowing inward towards the centre in either a clockwise direction (southern hemisphere) or counterclockwise (northern hemisphere).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">\n        <a href="#cite_note-66">\n         [66]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Although\n       <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">\n        cyclones\n       </a>\n       can take an enormous toll in lives and personal property, they may be important factors in the precipitation regimes of places they impact, as they may bring much-needed precipitation to otherwise dry regions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_EPac_outlook_67-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-2005_EPac_outlook-67">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Areas in their path can receive a year\'s worth of rainfall from a tropical cyclone passage.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">\n        <a href="#cite_note-68">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Large-scale_geographical_distribution">\n        Large-scale geographical distribution\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Large-scale geographical distribution">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Earth_rainfall_climatology" title="Earth rainfall climatology">\n        Earth rainfall climatology\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       On the large scale, the highest precipitation amounts outside topography fall in the tropics, closely tied to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">\n        Intertropical Convergence Zone\n       </a>\n       , itself the ascending branch of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Hadley_cell" title="Hadley cell">\n        Hadley cell\n       </a>\n       . Mountainous locales near the equator in Colombia are amongst the wettest places on Earth.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCDCxrain_69-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NCDCxrain-69">\n         [69]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       North and south of this are regions of descending air that form\n       <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">\n        subtropical ridges\n       </a>\n       where precipitation is low;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">\n        <a href="#cite_note-70">\n         [70]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       the land surface underneath is usually arid, which forms most of the Earth\'s deserts.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">\n        <a href="#cite_note-71">\n         [71]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       An exception to this rule is in Hawaii, where upslope flow due to the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_wind" title="Trade wind">\n        trade winds\n       </a>\n       lead to one of the wettest locations on Earth.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">\n        <a href="#cite_note-72">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Otherwise, the flow of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">\n        Westerlies\n       </a>\n       into the Rocky Mountains lead to the wettest, and at elevation snowiest,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">\n        <a href="#cite_note-73">\n         [73]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       locations within\n       <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">\n        North America\n       </a>\n       . In\n       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">\n        Asia\n       </a>\n       during the wet season, the flow of moist air into the\n       <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">\n        Himalayas\n       </a>\n       leads to some of the greatest rainfall amounts measured on Earth in northeast\n       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">\n        India\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">\n        Measurement\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Measurement">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">\n        Rain gauge\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Disdrometer" title="Disdrometer">\n        Disdrometer\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Snow_gauge" title="Snow gauge">\n        Snow gauge\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1156" data-file-width="544" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg/130px-250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg/195px-250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg/260px-250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg 2x" width="130"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Standard rain gauge\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The standard way of measuring rainfall or snowfall is the standard rain gauge, which can be found in 100\xc2\xa0mm (4\xc2\xa0in) plastic and 200\xc2\xa0mm (8\xc2\xa0in) metal varieties.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">\n        <a href="#cite_note-74">\n         [74]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The inner cylinder is filled by 25\xc2\xa0mm (1\xc2\xa0in) of rain, with overflow flowing into the outer cylinder. Plastic gauges have markings on the inner cylinder down to 0.25\xc2\xa0mm (0.01\xc2\xa0in) resolution, while metal gauges require use of a stick designed with the appropriate 0.25\xc2\xa0mm (0.01\xc2\xa0in) markings. After the inner cylinder is filled, the amount inside it is discarded, then filled with the remaining rainfall in the outer cylinder until all the fluid in the outer cylinder is gone, adding to the overall total until the outer cylinder is empty. These gauges are used in the winter by removing the funnel and inner cylinder and allowing snow and freezing rain to collect inside the outer cylinder. Some add anti-freeze to their gauge so they do not have to melt the snow or ice that falls into the gauge.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">\n        <a href="#cite_note-75">\n         [75]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Once the snowfall/ice is finished accumulating, or as 300\xc2\xa0mm (12\xc2\xa0in) is approached, one can either bring it inside to melt, or use lukewarm water to fill the inner cylinder with in order to melt the frozen precipitation in the outer cylinder, keeping track of the warm fluid added, which is subsequently subtracted from the overall total once all the ice/snow is melted.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">\n        <a href="#cite_note-76">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Other types of gauges include the popular wedge gauge (the cheapest rain gauge and most fragile), the tipping bucket rain gauge, and the weighing rain gauge.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">\n        <a href="#cite_note-77">\n         [77]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The wedge and tipping bucket gauges will have problems with snow. Attempts to compensate for snow/ice by warming the tipping bucket meet with limited success, since snow may sublimate if the gauge is kept much above freezing. Weighing gauges with antifreeze should do fine with snow, but again, the funnel needs to be removed before the event begins. For those looking to measure rainfall the most inexpensively, a can that is cylindrical with straight sides will act as a rain gauge if left out in the open, but its accuracy will depend on what ruler is used to measure the rain with. Any of the above rain gauges can be made at home, with enough know-how.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">\n        <a href="#cite_note-78">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       When a precipitation measurement is made, various networks exist across the United States and elsewhere where rainfall measurements can be submitted through the Internet, such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_Collaborative_Rain,_Hail_and_Snow_network" title="Community Collaborative Rain, Hail and Snow network">\n        CoCoRAHS\n       </a>\n       or GLOBE.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">\n        <a href="#cite_note-79">\n         [79]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">\n        <a href="#cite_note-80">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       If a network is not available in the area where one lives, the nearest local weather office will likely be interested in the measurement.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">\n        <a href="#cite_note-81">\n         [81]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrometeor_definition">\n        Hydrometeor definition\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hydrometeor definition">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       A concept used in precipitation measurement is the hydrometeor. Bits of liquid or solid water in the atmosphere are known as hydrometeors. Formations due to condensation, such as clouds,\n       <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">\n        haze\n       </a>\n       , fog, and mist, are composed of hydrometeors. All precipitation types are made up of hydrometeors by definition, including virga, which is precipitation which evaporates before reaching the ground. Particles blown from the Earth\'s surface by wind, such as blowing snow and blowing sea spray, are also hydrometeors.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">\n        <a href="#cite_note-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_estimates">\n        Satellite estimates\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Satellite estimates">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Although surface precipitation gauges are considered the standard for measuring precipitation, there are many areas in which their use is not feasible. This includes the vast expanses of ocean and remote land areas. In other cases, social, technical or administrative issues prevent the dissemination of gauge observations. As a result, the modern global record of precipitation largely depends on satellite observations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">\n        <a href="#cite_note-83">\n         [83]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Satellite sensors work by remotely sensing precipitation\xe2\x80\x94recording various parts of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">\n        electromagnetic spectrum\n       </a>\n       that theory and practice show are related to the occurrence and intensity of precipitation. The sensors are almost exclusively passive, recording what they see, similar to a camera, in contrast to active sensors (\n       <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">\n        radar\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">\n        lidar\n       </a>\n       ) that send out a signal and detect its impact on the area being observed.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Satellite sensors now in practical use for precipitation fall into two categories. Thermal infrared (\n       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">\n        IR\n       </a>\n       ) sensors record a channel around 11 micron wavelength and primarily give information about cloud tops. Due to the typical structure of the atmosphere, cloud-top temperatures are approximately inversely related to cloud-top heights, meaning colder clouds almost always occur at higher altitudes. Further, cloud tops with a lot of small-scale variation are likely to be more vigorous than smooth-topped clouds. Various mathematical schemes, or algorithms, use these and other properties to estimate precipitation from the IR data.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">\n        <a href="#cite_note-84">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The second category of sensor channels is in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">\n        microwave\n       </a>\n       part of the electromagnetic spectrum. The frequencies in use range from about 10 gigahertz to a few hundred GHz. Channels up to about 37\xc2\xa0GHz primarily provide information on the liquid hydrometeors (rain and drizzle) in the lower parts of clouds, with larger amounts of liquid emitting higher amounts of microwave radiant energy. Channels above 37\xc2\xa0GHz display emission signals, but are dominated by the action of solid hydrometeors (snow, graupel, etc.) to scatter microwave radiant energy. Satellites such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Rainfall_Measuring_Mission" title="Tropical Rainfall Measuring Mission">\n        Tropical Rainfall Measuring Mission\n       </a>\n       (TRMM) and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Global_Precipitation_Measurement" title="Global Precipitation Measurement">\n        Global Precipitation Measurement\n       </a>\n       (GPM) mission employ microwave sensors to form precipitation estimates.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Additional sensor channels and products have been demonstrated to provide additional useful information including visible channels, additional IR channels, water vapor channels and atmospheric sounding retrievals. However, most precipitation data sets in current use do not employ these data sources.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">\n        <a href="#cite_note-85">\n         [85]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_data_sets">\n        Satellite data sets\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Satellite data sets">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The IR estimates have rather low skill at short time and space scales, but are available very frequently (15 minutes or more often) from satellites in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geosynchronous" title="Geosynchronous">\n        geosynchronous\n       </a>\n       Earth orbit. IR works best in cases of deep, vigorous convection\xe2\x80\x94such as the tropics\xe2\x80\x94and becomes progressively less useful in areas where stratiform (layered) precipitation dominates, especially in mid- and high-latitude regions. The more-direct physical connection between hydrometeors and microwave channels gives the microwave estimates greater skill on short time and space scales than is true for IR. However, microwave sensors fly only on low Earth orbit satellites, and there are few enough of them that the average time between observations exceeds three hours. This several-hour interval is insufficient to adequately document precipitation because of the transient nature of most precipitation systems as well as the inability of a single satellite to appropriately capture the typical daily cycle of precipitation at a given location.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Since the late 1990s, several algorithms have been developed to combine precipitation data from multiple satellites\' sensors, seeking to emphasize the strengths and minimize the weaknesses of the individual input data sets. The goal is to provide "best" estimates of precipitation on a uniform time/space grid, usually for as much of the globe as possible. In some cases the long-term homogeneity of the dataset is emphasized, which is the\n       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_Data_Record&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Climate Data Record (page does not exist)">\n        Climate Data Record\n       </a>\n       standard.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In other cases, the goal is producing the best instantaneous satellite estimate, which is the High Resolution Precipitation Product approach. In either case, of course, the less-emphasized goal is also considered desirable. One key result of the multi-satellite studies is that including even a small amount of surface gauge data is very useful for controlling the biases that are endemic to satellite estimates. The difficulties in using gauge data are that 1) their availability is limited, as noted above, and 2) the best analyses of gauge data take two months or more after the observation time to undergo the necessary transmission, assembly, processing and quality control. Thus, precipitation estimates that include gauge data tend to be produced further after the observation time than the no-gauge estimates. As a result, while estimates that include gauge data may provide a more accurate depiction of the "true" precipitation, they are generally not suited for real- or near-real-time applications.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The work described has resulted in a variety of datasets possessing different formats, time/space grids, periods of record and regions of coverage, input datasets, and analysis procedures, as well as many different forms of dataset version designators.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">\n        <a href="#cite_note-86">\n         [86]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In many cases, one of the modern multi-satellite data sets is the best choice for general use.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Return_period">\n        Return period\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Return period">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/100-year_flood" title="100-year flood">\n        100-year flood\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The likelihood or probability of an event with a specified intensity and duration, is called the\n       <a href="/wiki/Return_period" title="Return period">\n        return period\n       </a>\n       or frequency.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">\n        <a href="#cite_note-87">\n         [87]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The intensity of a storm can be predicted for any return period and storm duration, from charts based on historic data for the location.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">\n        <a href="#cite_note-88">\n         [88]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The term\n       <i>\n        1 in 10\xc2\xa0year storm\n       </i>\n       describes a rainfall event which is rare and is only likely to occur once every 10\xc2\xa0years, so it has a 10\xc2\xa0percent likelihood any given year. The rainfall will be greater and the flooding will be worse than the worst storm expected in any single year. The term\n       <i>\n        1 in 100\xc2\xa0year storm\n       </i>\n       describes a rainfall event which is extremely rare and which will occur with a likelihood of only once in a century, so has a 1\xc2\xa0percent likelihood in any given year. The rainfall will be extreme and flooding to be worse than a 1 in 10 year event. As with all probability events, it is possible though unlikely to have two "1 in 100\xc2\xa0Year Storms" in a single year.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">\n        <a href="#cite_note-89">\n         [89]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span id="Role_in_K\xc3\xb6ppen_climate_classification">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_K.C3.B6ppen_climate_classification">\n        Role in K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Precipitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Role in K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">\n        K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">\n        <a class="image" Csa\n             </a>\n            </div>\n            <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">\n             <span climate">\n              Cwb\n             </a>\n            </div>\n           </td>\n           <td width="5">\n           </td>\n           <td>\n            <div class="legend"
